Why the Muqallid's Faith is Beneficial
Nasafi argues that the muqallid's faith benefits due to absence of three hindering factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the faith of a muqallid (blind follower) is considered beneficial and accepted by Allah according to Imam Nasafi's argument.

Core Idea
Faith accepted if free from three hindrances: fear of punishment, compulsion, or deathbed belief.

Imam Nasafi addresses a key concern: does blind faith (taqlid) in matters of belief count? Critics argue it lacks the hardship of evidence-seeking. Nasafi defends the muqallid by showing that his faith is still a sincere act of drawing near to Allah, absent in three cases where faith is not accepted. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three hindering factors
Read the passage carefully to find the three conditions that prevent faith from being beneficial: faith to avoid imminent punishment, faith under compulsion, and faith at the point of death (soul departing).
These factors are mentioned as exceptions where scriptural texts indicate faith is not accepted.
2
See how these factors are absent in the muqallid
Now see how the muqallid's faith is entirely different: it is an act of taqarrub (drawing near to Allah), not out of fear; there is no compulsion; and his soul is still in his control.
3
Understand the absolute promise of reward
Nasafi argues that Allah's promise of reward for faith applies universally, regardless of hardship. The muqallid performed faith, so he deserves reward by virtue of the promise.
4
Contrast with the exceptional cases
The passage contrasts the muqallid with those who believed only when seeing punishment (like Pharaoh), who were compelled, or whose souls had left. Their faith is not accepted due to these factors.
5
Conclude the benefit of muqallid's faith
Since all three hindering factors are absent, the muqallid's faith retains its benefit and is accepted by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العذاب، أو لأنه عند تعلق العذاب الدنيوي به وهو مقدمة لعذاب الآخرة، إذ يموت بعذاب الدنيا، فينقل إلى عذاب الآخرة، خرجت نفسه من يديه إذ لا تقدر بعد ذلك على التصرف في نفسه، والاستمتاع بها، وشيء من هذه المعاني لم يوجد في حق المقلد، إذ إيمانه كان تقربا إلى الله تعالى، وطلبا لمرضاته، لا لدفع عذاب متوجه إليه، متشبث به إذ لا عذاب توجه إليه أو تشبث به، وكذلك لم يكن يلجأ إلى إيمانه مضطرا لانعدام سبب الاضطرار، وكذلك كانت نفسه في يديه لم يخرج منها وقد حصل منه الإيمان والله تعالى وعد الثواب على الإيمان، والثواب لا ينال إلا بفضل الله ووعده، فإذا وعد الثواب على فعل ينال فاعله الثواب سواء تحمل المشقة أو لم يتحمل، فلا ينعدم ثواب إيمان المقلد وإن لم يتحمل مشقة لتناول مطلق الوعد إياه، فأما إيمان من آمن دفعا للعذاب عن نفسه، أو كان ملجأ إليه، أو بعدما خرجت نفسه عن يده، فقد ورد النص بخروجه عن كونه نافعا، فقلنا بأنه لا ينفع، وفي الملقد اندفع هذا النص، وبقي مطلق الوعد على الإيمان، والشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله بين هذه المعاني الثلاثة لحرمان من آمن عند معاينة العذاب نفع الإيمان، فعلى هذا يكون هذا إشارة إلى أن المقلد لا يحرم نفعه لانعدام هذه المعاني عنه، والله الموفق.
The punishment, or because when the worldly punishment attaches to him and it is a prelude to the punishment of the Hereafter, since he dies by the punishment of this world and is transferred to the punishment of the Hereafter, his soul departed from his hands, for he is no longer able to control himself or enjoy it. None of these meanings existed in the case of the muqallid (blind follower), for his faith was an approach to Allah Most High and a seeking of His pleasure, not to repel punishment directed toward him and clinging to him, since no punishment was directed toward him or clung to him. Likewise, he did not resort to his faith under compulsion, due to the absence of a cause for compulsion. Likewise, his soul was in his hands, not having departed from them, and faith occurred from him. Allah Most High has promised reward for faith, and reward is only attained by Allah's grace and His promise. So when He promised reward for an action, its doer attains the reward, whether he bears hardship or does not bear it. Thus the reward of the muqallid's faith is not nullified, even if he does not bear hardship, because the absolute promise encompasses him. As for the faith of one who believed to repel punishment from himself, or was compelled to it, or after his soul had departed from his hand, the text has come that it is considered not beneficial, so we said it does not benefit. In the case of the compelled one, this text is repelled, and the absolute promise for faith remains. Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) explained these three meanings for the deprivation of benefit of faith for one who believes at the sight of punishment. On this basis, this indicates that the muqallid is not deprived of its benefit, due to the absence of these meanings from him. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that the faith of the muqallid (one who believes without independent evidence) is beneficial and accepted by Allah. The author begins by describing three conditions that prevent faith from being beneficial: 1) when the person believes only to avoid punishment that has already attached to him, 2) when he is under compulsion (idṭirār), and 3) when his soul has departed from his control (i.e., at the moment of death when the angel of death is present). He then argues that none of these conditions apply to the muqallid. The muqallid's faith is an act of drawing near to Allah (taqarrub) out of sincere desire for His pleasure, not out of fear of imminent punishment. He is not compelled, because there is no cause forcing him to believe. He is in full control of himself. Therefore, the universal promise of reward for faith applies to him, regardless of whether he undertook the hardship of seeking evidence. The author contrasts this with those who believe only when they see punishment (like Pharaoh), who were compelled, or whose souls had left. In those cases, scriptural texts indicate that their faith is not accepted. But for the muqallid, the word of Allah promising reward for faith remains applicable. The author attributes the clarification of these three hindering meanings to Shaykh Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi). Thus, the muqallid's faith is beneficial because all three obstacles are absent.

مقلد
عذاب
تقرب
اضطرار
مطلق الوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muqallid's faith is invalid because he lacks independent evidence (nazar).
Correction: According to Nasafi, the muqallid's faith is still accepted because it is not tainted by the three hindering factors (fear, compulsion, deathbed). The promise of reward applies absolutely.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three factors that prevent faith from being beneficial according to this passage?
A) Believing only to repel punishment that is already imminent
B) Being compelled to believe
C) Believing out of sincere desire for Allah's pleasure
D) Believing after the soul has left the body or is about to leave

Key Takeaway
The faith of a muqallid is beneficial because it lacks the three hindering factors (fear, compulsion, deathbed) that would otherwise negate its reward, and Allah's promise of reward for faith applies absolutely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe,' is your faith a free choice seeking Allah's pleasure, or is it driven by a hidden fear of punishment? How can you purify your intention today?
?
This passage shows that even a blind follower's faith is accepted. Does that comfort you or urge you to seek deeper knowledge? What step will you take this week to move from taqlid to tahqīq?
?
Imagine yourself in a moment of ease versus a moment of crisis: is your faith consistent? If you find that you only turn to Allah when in distress, what does that reveal about the sincerity of your faith? Make a concrete plan to worship Him in times of comfort.

beginner

The Ruling of the Muqallid
Nasafi states the madhhab that the muqallid is a believer and lists its supporters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that a muqallid (one who believes without evidence) is a believer with the same ultimate destiny as any sinful Muslim, and the consensus of major scholars supporting this view.

Core Idea
The muqallid is a believer—his faith is valid despite lacking proof.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabirat al-Adilla addresses a crucial question: Is someone who believes in Allah and His Messenger without engaging in rational reflection (nazar) a true believer? Nasafi affirms that such a person is indeed a believer, though he is sinful for abandoning the duty to reflect. He then lists a broad consensus of scholars to show that this is not a fringe view but the position of the great Imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own wording settle before analysis.
Notice the confident opening: 'And this madhhab is that...'
2
Identify the key claim
The passage clearly states that the muqallid is a believer (mu'min) and that Islamic rulings apply to him. This is the central point.
3
Notice the nuance: obedience vs disobedience
The muqallid is obedient in his belief and actions, but disobedient in abandoning reflection (nazar) and inference (istidlal). This duality is important for understanding his status.
4
Look at the list of supporters
Nasafi names many scholars, including the four Imams and early theologians. This shows the view is well-supported.
Consider why Nasafi would include such a list—to strengthen the authority of the position.
5
Reflect on the final outcome
The muqallid's ultimate destiny is Paradise, though he may be punished temporarily for his sin. This aligns with the status of any sinful Muslim (fasiq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا المذهب أن المقلد الذي لا دليل معه مؤمن، وحكم الإسلام له لازم، وهو مطيع الله تعالى باعتقاده وسائر طاعاته، وإن كان عاصيا بترك النظر والاستدلال، وحكمه حكم غيره من فساق أهل الملة من جواز مغفرته أو تعذيبه بقدر ذنبه، وعاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة هذا القول محكى عن أبي حنيفة الله والثوري ومالك والأوزاعي والشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل وأهل الظاهر، ومن المتكلمين عن عبد الله بن سعيد القطان، والحارث بن أسد، وعبد العزيز بن يحيى المكي، وتلخيص مذهب أبي حنيفة، وأصحابنا الله رحمهم بينت والله الموفق.
And this madhhab (position) is that the muqallid who has no proof with him is a believer, and the judgment of Islam is binding upon him. He is obedient to Allah Most High by his belief and all his other acts of obedience, although he is disobedient by abandoning reflection (nazar) and inference (istidlal). His judgment is the same as that of other sinners (fussaq) of the religious community: it is possible for him to be forgiven or punished to the extent of his sin, and his final outcome is Paradise without fail. This statement is narrated from Abu Hanifah (may Allah have mercy on him), al-Thawri, Malik, al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and the Zahiris; and from the mutakallimun (speculative theologians) from 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, al-Harith ibn Asad, and 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Yahya al-Makki. And the summary of the madhhab of Abu Hanifah and our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) I have explained. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the madhhab (school position) regarding the muqallid (the one who believes without evidence). The author, al-Nasafi, declares that the muqallid is indeed a believer (mu'min) and that all the legal rulings of Islam apply to him. He is considered obedient (muti') to Allah through his belief and all his other acts of worship, even though he is simultaneously disobedient ('asi) for failing to engage in nazar (reflection) and istidlal (inference), which are duties according to the Maturidi school. The author then equates the muqallid's status with that of any other sinner (fasiq) among the Muslims: he may be forgiven by Allah's grace or punished according to his sin, but his ultimate destination is Paradise without doubt. After stating the ruling, the author provides an impressive list of scholars who held this position, including the four great Imams (Abu Hanifah, Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal), other early jurists like al-Thawri and al-Awza'i, the Zahiri school, and three notable mutakallimun: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan (Ibn Kullab), al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Makki. The author concludes by saying that he has already explained the summary of Abu Hanifah's madhhab and that of his fellow Maturidi scholars.

The Ruling on the Sinful Believer and Its Proponents
Imam Nasafi states the ruling on the sinful believer and lists its proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah position on the ultimate fate of the sinful believer and recognize the early authorities who affirmed this view.

Core Idea
The sinful believer's final abode is Paradise, though he may be punished for his sins.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabirat al-Adilla tackles a key point of contention between Ahl al-Sunnah and their opponents (Khawarij, Mu'tazila). Nasafi first states the ruling, then supports it by listing a chain of early Sunni imams who held the same view. Reading this passage will help you see how the Sunni tradition maintained hope for sinful believers while affirming divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the ruling first, then the list of authorities.

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the grave sinner (fasiq) from the community of Muslims. It begins by equating the fasiq's ruling with that of other sinners: his fate is not predetermined—either forgiveness or punishment is possible depending on God's will, but ultimately he will enter Paradise. This is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah against the Khawarij and Mu‘tazila. The author then lists numerous early imams who held this view: Abu Hanifa, Sufyan al-Thawri, Malik ibn Anas, al-Awza‘i, al-Shafi‘i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and the Zahiri school; from the theologians, ‘Abdullah ibn Sa‘id al-Qattan, al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and ‘Abd al-‘Aziz al-Makki. He concludes by stating this is the summary of Abu Hanifa’s school and his followers. The final sentence suggests the author has clarified the matter, relying on Allah’s guidance.

The Ash'ari View on Faith Without Rational Proof
Al-Nasafi presents the Ash'ari position on the status of one who believes without rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the faith of a person who believes without rational proof, and how this person is ultimately saved despite lacking full belief.

Core Idea
A believer without rational proof is not perfect but still saved.

The Ash'ari school holds a nuanced view on faith that requires rational proof for foundational beliefs. However, a person who affirms the truth without such proof is not considered a disbeliever but a disobedient Muslim. This passage from al-Nasafi, quoting al-Baghdadi, clarifies this position and its implications for salvation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of rational proof
Read the condition that rational proof is needed for each foundational issue, but only internal knowledge is required.
Note that the proof must be rational (dalil 'aqli), not just transmitted.
2
Note what is not required
The passage clarifies that verbal expression and debating ability are not required; only heartfelt knowledge suffices.
3
Grasp the final status according to al-Baghdadi
Al-Baghdadi explains that such a person is not a kafir due to having tasdiq (affirmation), even though he is disobedient for abandoning reflection. His final outcome is Paradise.
This distinguishes between a perfect believer and a sinful Muslim who is ultimately saved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور من مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري أنه لا يكون مؤمنا ما لم يعتقد كل مسألة من مسائل الأصول عن دليل عقلي غير أن الشرط أن يعرف ذلك بقلبه ولا يشترط أن يعبر عن ذلك بلسانه وأن يكون بصيرا بمجادلة الخصوم قادرا على دفع ما يورد عليه من الإشكال وإبطال ما توجه عليه من الشبه، قال عبد القاهر البغدادي: إن هذا وإن لم يكن مؤمنا عند الأشعري على الإطلاق غير أنه ليس بكافر عنده لوجود ما يضاد الكفر والشرك من هذا الشخص وهو التصديق، وهو عاصي بتركه النظر والاستدلال، والله تعالى فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه وأدخله الجنة، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه، فصار عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة كسائر عصاة المسلمين.
And the well-known [view] from the school of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that he is not a believer as long as he has not believed every issue of the foundational matters from a rational proof, except that the condition is that he knows that in his heart, and it is not required that he express it with his tongue, and that he be insightful in debating opponents, able to repel what is advanced against him of difficulties and to invalidate what is directed against him of ambiguities. Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi said: This [person] – even if he is not a believer according to al-Ash'ari in an absolute sense – is nevertheless not a disbeliever in his view, because there exists in this person what contradicts disbelief and polytheism, namely, affirmation (tasdiq). He is disobedient for abandoning reflection and reasoning. Allah Most High has the will concerning him: if He wills, He pardons him and admits him to Paradise; if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin. Then his final outcome will be Paradise necessarily, like the rest of the disobedient Muslims.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known Ash'ari position on the person who does not reason rationally. Al-Ash'ari holds that such a person is not a fully-fledged believer (mu'min) because he lacks rational proof for each foundational issue. However, the requirement is only internal knowledge, not verbal expression or debating ability. Then al-Baghdadi clarifies: according to al-Ash'ari, this person is not a kafir because he possesses tasdiq (affirmation), which contradicts kufr and shirk. His sin is abandoning reflection (nazar) and reasoning (istidlal). He is under Allah's will; he may be forgiven and enter Paradise directly, or be punished temporarily for his sin, but his final destination is Paradise like other sinful Muslims. Thus, al-Ash'ari distinguishes between being a perfect believer and being a sinner who ultimately attains salvation.

Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari declares a muqallid (one who believes without proof) to be a disbeliever.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari considers such a person not a perfect believer but not a kafir; he is a disobedient Muslim whose final abode is Paradise.

Mu'tazila Views on the Muqallid's Faith
Al-Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila disagreement on the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazila positions on the faith of the muqallid and the underlying reasoning about rational proof and major sins.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, faith without proof is either no faith, a sin, or an intermediate state.

Al-Nasafi here reports a famous internal disagreement within the Mu'tazila school. The question is: what is the status of a person who believes in Allah and His messenger but does so without rational proof? The Mu'tazila differ among themselves, but all agree that mere blind acceptance is insufficient for true faith. This passage captures their diverse views and the logic behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the different voices: the majority, Abu Hashim, and others. Let the text speak before you analyze.
2
Identify the three Mu'tazila positions
The passage presents three distinct views on the muqallid. Find each one and note who holds it. The majority says he is not a believer; Abu Hashim says he is a kafir; others say he is in the intermediate position.
The 'others' are implied by the attribution to Abu Hashim.
3
Examine the common condition
After stating the disagreement, the passage says that according to all Mu'tazila, one is judged to have faith only if he knows by proof and can debate opponents. Why do you think they add this condition?
This condition explains why the muqallid is deficient.
4
Understand the reasoning about sin
The passage then explains the source of disagreement: abandoning reasoning (tark al-istidlal) is a major sin (kabira). The Mu'tazila hold that a major sin expels from faith. Note how this connects to their positions.
For Abu Hashim, the sin makes him a kafir; for others, it places him in an intermediate state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيمان المقلد عند المعتزلة ومناقشة كلامهم واختلفت المعتزلة في هذا المعتقد لا عن دليل، قال عامتهم : إنه ليس بمؤمن. وقال أبو هاشم منهم: إنه كافر، كذا حكى عنهم عبد القاهر البغدادي، ونسبته القول إلى أبي هاشم بأنه كافر دليل أن عند غيره من المعتزلة ليس هذا بكافر ولا مؤمن، بل هو على المنزلة بين المنزلتين، ثم عند جميع المعتزلة إنما يحكم بإيمانه إذا عرف ما يجب اعتقاده بالدليل على وجه يمكنه مجادلة الخصوم وحل ما يورد عليه من الإشكال، حتى إنه لو عجز عن شيء من ذلك لم يحكم بإسلامه، ثم هذا الاختلاف بين المعتزلة إنما نشأ عن أن هذا المعتقد لا عن دليل عاص بتركه الاستدلال، وترك الاستدلال كبير، والكبيرة الطارئة على الإيمان تخرج صاحبها عن الإيمان، فمنعت المقارنة عن الدخول فيه، وعند أبي هاشم توبة الكافر وغيره عن الكفر أو عن معصية مع بقائه على معصية أخرى صحيحة وإن كان
The faith of the muqallid (blind follower) according to the Mu'tazila and a discussion of their statement. The Mu'tazila differed concerning this one who believes without proof. The majority of them said: He is not a believer. And Abu Hashim among them said: He is a disbeliever (kafir). Thus Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi narrated from them. And his attribution of the statement to Abu Hashim that he is a kafir is evidence that according to others among the Mu'tazila, he is neither a kafir nor a believer, but he is in the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Then according to all Mu'tazila, one only judges him to have faith if he knows what he must believe by proof in a manner that enables him to debate opponents and resolve difficulties raised against him, such that if he is unable to do any of that, his Islam is not judged. Then this disagreement among the Mu'tazila arose only because this one who believes without proof is disobedient for abandoning reasoning, and abandoning reasoning is a major sin (kabira), and a major sin that befalls faith expels its possessor from faith, so it prevents the conjunction [of faith] from entering it. And according to Abu Hashim, the repentance of a disbeliever and others from disbelief or from a sin while remaining upon another sin is valid, even if...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila positions on the one who believes without rational proof (muqallid). The majority say he is not a believer. Abu Hashim says he is a kafir. Al-Baghdadi's narration indicates that others among the Mu'tazila consider him neither kafir nor believer, but in the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Then the passage explains a common condition among all Mu'tazila: one is only judged to have faith if he knows by proof in a way that enables him to debate and resolve doubts. If he cannot, his Islam is not judged. The reason for their disagreement is that abandoning reasoning (tark al-istidlal) is a major sin (kabira), and a major sin that occurs upon faith expels one from faith. So the sin prevents faith from entering. Finally, Abu Hashim's view on repentance is noted: repentance from disbelief or from a sin is valid even if one remains upon another sin. The text ends mid-sentence.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

Ash'ari Ruling on the Muqallid's Faith
Defines the Ash'ari position on the muqallid as faithful but disobedient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that a muqallid (one who believes without reasoning) is not a disbeliever but is sinful, and that his final end is Paradise.

Core Idea
Tasdiq (affirmation) saves from disbelief, but neglecting reasoning is sinful.

Imam al-Ash'ari teaches that the essence of faith is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart). A person who has this affirmation is not a disbeliever, even if he failed to arrive at it through proper inquiry. However, that failure is a sin. The following passage from Abu'l-Mu'in al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla lays out this nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the words of the author sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three key claims about the muqallid: (a) he is not a believer absolutely, (b) but he is not a disbeliever, (c) he is disobedient. Notice the reasoning for each.
The presence of tasdiq (affirmation) prevents disbelief.
3
Understand the role of tasdiq
Tasdiq is what contradicts kufr and shirk. Even without reasoning, it keeps the person within the fold of Islam. Reflect on why this is the core of faith for Ash'ari.
This is a key principle in Ash'ari theology.
4
Contemplate the final outcome
The passage ends with the muqallid's fate: he is subject to Allah's will but ultimately enters Paradise. Compare this to other sinful Muslims.
Disobedience does not nullify faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وإن لم يكن مؤمنا عند الأشعري على الإطلاق غير أنه ليس بكافر عنده لوجود ما يضاد الكفر والشرك من هذا الشخص وهو التصديق، وهو عاص بتركه النظر والاستدلال، والله تعالى فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه وأدخله الجنة، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه، فصار عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة كسائر عصاة المسلمين.
This [person] – even if he is not considered a believer at all according to Ash'ari – nevertheless, he is not a disbeliever in his [Ash'ari's] view, because there exists from this person that which contradicts disbelief and polytheism, namely tasdiq (affirmation). And he is disobedient by abandoning nazar (inquiry) and istidlal (deductive reasoning). And Allah Most High has the will concerning him: if He wills, He pardons him and admits him to Paradise; and if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin. Then his final end will inevitably be Paradise, like the rest of the sinful Muslims.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari ruling on a person who holds correct belief (tasdiq) but without having arrived at it through deliberate inquiry and reasoning. The author (Abu'l-Mu'in al-Nasafi) explains that according to Abu'l-Hasan al-Ash'ari, such a person is not a true believer (mu'min) in the full sense, because he has neglected the obligation to reflect. However, he is also not a disbeliever (kafir), because he possesses the essence of faith: tasdiq (affirmation of the heart). This tasdiq contradicts disbelief and polytheism. The person is therefore sinful (asi) for abandoning nazar and istidlal, but his sin does not expel him from the faith entirely. His fate rests with Allah's will: he may be pardoned or punished temporarily, but ultimately he will enter Paradise, just like any Muslim who commits major sins but dies with faith. The passage clarifies that the Ash'ari position distinguishes between the reality of iman (tasdiq) and the conditions for its full reward, affirming that tasdiq alone saves one from eternal punishment.

Key Terms
مؤمن
كافر
تصديق
نظر
استدلال
عاصي
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari considers the muqallid a kafir.
Correction: Ash'ari explicitly states the muqallid is not a kafir because he has tasdiq, though he is sinful.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, what is the status of a muqallid (one who believes without evidence)?
A) He is a kafir.
B) He is a true believer (mu'min) and will be rewarded directly.
C) He is not a kafir, but he is disobedient for abandoning inquiry, yet his end is Paradise.
D) He is in a state between belief and disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid is saved from eternal punishment by tasdiq, but is sinful for neglecting the duty to reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have tasdiq in your heart, but have you ever deliberately avoided deep reflection on your faith because it seemed difficult or unnecessary? This lesson calls that neglect sin. What will you do this week to fulfill the duty of nazar?
?
If you were to die tonight with only tasdiq but no genuine inquiry, would you be content with Allah's possible punishment for your neglect? What concrete step can you take today to move from muqallid to mu'min kamil?
?
The muqallid is saved from eternal fire but still faces accountability. Does this combination of mercy and warning trouble you? How does it change the way you view your own responsibility to seek understanding?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Mu'tazila Positions on the Muqallid's Faith
Enumerates three Mu'tazila views on the muqallid and their shared condition for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazila positions on the faith status of a person who holds correct belief without evidence, and the additional condition that faith is only judged when one can debate using evidence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila split into three camps on the muqallid, but all required evidential reasoning for true faith.

The Mu'tazila school was known for its rationalist approach to theology. When confronted with the case of a person who holds the correct beliefs but cannot produce evidence (the muqallid), they disagreed sharply. In this passage, we learn the three distinct positions held by different Mu'tazila scholars, as well as a common condition they all set for judging someone a believer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the passage and note the three different positions: the majority say 'not a believer', Abu Hashim says 'disbeliever', and others say 'neither believer nor disbeliever' (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn).
The third view is inferred from al-Baghdadi's report.
2
Understand the inference from al-Baghdadi
Notice how the author uses al-Baghdadi's attribution to Abu Hashim to deduce the existence of the intermediate view.
This shows how scholarly reports can reveal hidden positions.
3
Examine the shared condition
All Mu'tazila agree that true faith requires knowledge through evidence and the ability to debate opponents and resolve doubts. Without this capacity, one's Islam is not judged.
This condition explains why they did not simply consider the muqallid a believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفت المعتزلة في هذا المعتقد لا عن دليل، قال عامتهم: إنه ليس بمؤمن. وقال أبو هاشم منهم: إنه كافر. كذا حكى عنهم عبد القاهر البغدادي، ونسبته القول إلى أبي هاشم بأنه كافر دليل أن عند غيره من المعتزلة ليس هذا بكافر ولا مؤمن، بل هو على المنزلة بين المنزلتين. ثم عند جميع المعتزلة إنما يحكم بإيمانه إذا عرف ما يجب اعتقاده بالدليل على وجه يمكنه مجادلة الخصوم وحل ما يورد عليه من الإشكال، حتى إنه لو عجز عن شيء من ذلك لم يحكم بإسلامه. ثم هذا الاختلاف بين المعتزلة إنما نشأ عن أن هذا المعتقد لا عن دليل عاص بتركه الاستدلال، وترك الاستدلال كبير، والكبيرة الطارئة على الإيمان تخرج صاحبها عن الإيمان، فمنعت المقارنة عن الدخول فيه، وعند أبي هاشم توبة الكافر وغيره عن الكفر أو عن معصية مع بقائه على معصية أخرى صحيحة وإن كان تاب عن الكفر بقي فيه إذا لم يتب عن كبيرة وجدت منه، من غصب مال وأشباه ذلك وإن كان تاب عن معصيته بقي فيها إذا لم يتب عن كبيرة أخرى إن كان نشأ عن هذا، فإذا الاختلاف في هذه المسألة فرع فنتكلم في مسألة صاحب الكبيرة مع عامة المعتزلة، وفي مسألة صحة التوبة عن كبيرة مع بقائه على كبيرة أخرى مع أبي هاشم.
And the Mu'tazila differed concerning this person who holds belief not based on evidence. The majority of them said: He is not a believer. And Abu Hashim among them said: He is a disbeliever. Thus 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi narrated from them, and his attribution of the statement to Abu Hashim that he is a disbeliever indicates that according to others among the Mu'tazila, this [person] is neither a disbeliever nor a believer, but rather he is in the manzila bayna al-manzilatayn (the station between the two stations). Then, according to all Mu'tazila, judgment of his faith is only made when he knows what must be believed by means of evidence, in a way that enables him to argue with opponents and resolve difficulties presented to him; so much so that if he is incapable of any of that, his Islam is not judged. Then this disagreement among the Mu'tazila arose only because this person who holds belief without evidence is disobedient due to abandoning istidlal, and abandoning istidlal is a kabira (major sin), and a major sin that occurs upon faith expels its possessor from faith, and thus it prevented the conjunction [of faith] from entering into it. And according to Abu Hashim, repentance of a disbeliever and others from disbelief or from a sin while remaining upon another sin is valid, even if he repented from disbelief, he remains in it [i.e., the other sin] if he did not repent from a major sin that occurred from him, such as usurping wealth and the like. And if he repented from his sin, he remains in it if he did not repent from another major sin that arose from this. So the disagreement in this issue is a branch; we will then discuss the issue of the committer of a major sin with the generality of the Mu'tazila, and the issue of the validity of repentance from a major sin while remaining upon another major sin with Abu Hashim.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage reports and analyzes the Mu'tazila positions on the status of a person who holds correct belief but without evidence (muqallid). The author first states that the Mu'tazila disagreed: the majority said he is not a believer, while Abu Hashim said he is a disbeliever. An intermediate view (attributed to others) places him in the 'manzila bayna al-manzilatayn' (neither believer nor disbeliever). However, all Mu'tazila agree that a person is only judged as a believer when he knows the required beliefs through evidence and can engage in debate and resolve doubts. The root of their disagreement is that the muqallid is sinful for abandoning istidlal (deductive reasoning), and this sin is a major sin (kabira). For the Mu'tazila, a major sin committed after faith expels one from faith; hence the sin prevents faith from entering (or remaining). For Abu Hashim in particular, repentance from one major sin while persisting in another is valid, so the muqallid could be a disbeliever because he persists in the sin of abandoning istidlal. The author notes that the disagreement is a branch of the larger issue of the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira), which they will discuss with the general Mu'tazila, and the validity of repentance from one major sin while remaining on another, which they will discuss with Abu Hashim.

Key Terms
معتزلة
مقلد
دليل
أبو هاشم
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila considered the muqallid a kafir.
Correction: The majority said he is not a believer, and some placed him in an intermediate station (neither believer nor disbeliever). Only Abu Hashim said he is a kafir.

Checkpoint
What is the majority Mu'tazila view on the faith status of the muqallid?
A) He is a believer.
B) He is a disbeliever (kafir).
C) He is not a believer (but not necessarily a disbeliever).
D) He is in the intermediate station.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held three different positions on the muqallid's faith, but all agreed that true faith requires evidential knowledge and the ability to debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith were tested by your ability to defend it with evidence and resolve doubts, would you be found wanting? What does that say about the strength of your conviction?
?
You likely inherited your belief in Allah from your family. But have you ever personally examined the evidence for His existence? If not, are you any different from the muqallid the Mu'tazila criticized?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that faith must enable a person to argue against opponents. Think of someone who challenges your beliefs. Can you respond with clear reasoning? If not, what step will you take this week to equip yourself?

The Root Cause of the Mu'tazila Disagreement on the Muqallid
Explains the root cause of the Mu'tazila disagreement on the muqallid and details Abu Hashim's repentance theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila disagreed about the muqallid: because abandoning istidlal is a major sin, and they held that major sin expels from faith, and you will learn Abu Hashim's unique position on repentance from one major sin while persisting in another.

Core Idea
Abandoning evidence is a major sin that, for the Mu'tazila, can remove one from faith altogether.

Having learned the three positions of the Mu'tazila on the muqallid, we now delve into the reasoning behind their disagreement. The author explains that the muqallid's failure to engage in istidlal (deductive reasoning) is considered a major sin (kabira) by the Mu'tazila, and they believe that a major sin committed after faith expels a person from faith. The author also introduces Abu Hashim's nuanced view on repentance, which further explains his position that the muqallid is a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the root cause
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the cause: the muqallid's abandonment of istidlal is a major sin.
The sin is described as 'kabira' (major sin).
2
Understand the effect of a major sin on faith
According to the Mu'tazila, a major sin that occurs upon faith expels its possessor from faith. This means the muqallid's sin prevents him from being a believer.
This is a core Mu'tazila doctrine.
3
Examine Abu Hashim's repentance theory
Abu Hashim held that repentance from one major sin is valid even if one persists in another major sin. This explains his view that the muqallid is a disbeliever, because he persists in the sin of abandoning istidlal.
The author will later discuss this topic separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Evidence and Benefit in Faith
Nasafi presents and refutes the objection that faith without evidence lacks reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether faith without evidence is beneficial and the Ahl al-Sunna position that reward corresponds to affirmation itself by God's favor.

Core Idea
True faith is beneficial, but evidence-seeking hardship determines reward.

This passage presents an objection from those who claim that faith without evidence is not beneficial. Nasafi explains their reasoning—reward is tied to hardship, and hardship lies in seeking evidence, not in affirmation itself—and then reaffirms the Sunni position that reward is by God's favor and corresponds to affirmation itself, with exceptions for compelled belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the objection and the response structure.
The passage starts with 'فإن قالوا' indicating an objection.
2
Identify the objectors' argument
The objectors claim that belief without evidence is not beneficial because benefit (reward) is tied to hardship, and hardship is in reflecting on evidence, not in the affirmation itself.
Note the key term 'المشقة' (hardship).
3
Understand the Sunni response
Nasafi counters by stating the Ahl al-Sunna position: reward is by God's favor and corresponds to affirmation itself, except for cases like faith at the moment of despair or compulsion.
This response emphasizes God's grace over strict merit.
4
Reflect on the exception
Consider why faith at the point of witnessing punishment or when compelled is not beneficial according to the objectors, and why it is excluded in the Sunni view.
This exception is grounded in earlier discussion (ما بينا).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن هذا الاعتقاد ليس نفسه بإيمان أو هو إيمان غير أنه ليس بنافع ما لم يكن مبنيا على الدليل، فحينئذ يقع بيننا وبينهم كلام في أصل المسألة، ثم هؤلاء الذين يشترطون كون الاعتقاد والتصديق مبنيًّا على الدليل لكونه نافعا إن زعموا أن خروجه عن النفع إنما يكون لانعدام تحمل المشقة، والثواب يقابل تحمل المشقة، ومباشرة المكروه طلبا لمرضاة الله ولا مشقة في نفس التصديق بل المشقة في الوصول إلى التصديق بحصول العلم بالتأمل في الدليل، ولهذا لا ينفع التصديق الحاصل عند اليأس وفي الآخرة، فالكلام لعامة الفقهاء ومتكلمي أهل السنة منهم على ما مر من نيل الثواب بوعد الله تعالى تفضلا منه فيكون بمقابلة ما قابله به وجعل ذلك بمقابلة التصديق فيقابله، إلا ما خص منه كإيمان من عاين العذاب أو كان مضطرا فيه على ما بينا
For if they say: 'This belief itself is not faith, or it is faith but it is not beneficial unless it is based on evidence,' then at that point a discussion occurs between us and them on the root of the matter. Then those who stipulate that belief and affirmation be based on evidence because it is beneficial, if they claim that its lacking benefit is only due to absence of bearing hardship, and reward corresponds to bearing hardship, and undertaking the disliked seeking the pleasure of God, and there is no hardship in the affirmation itself, but the hardship is in arriving at the affirmation through acquiring knowledge by reflecting on the evidence, and for this reason affirmation attained at the point of despair and in the afterlife is not beneficial. So the statement according to the generality of jurists and theologians of Ahl al-Sunna among them is as previously mentioned: attainment of reward by God's promise as a favor, so it corresponds to what it is made to correspond to, and that is made to correspond to affirmation, so it corresponds to it, except what is excluded from it, like the faith of one who witnesses the punishment or was compelled in it, as we explained.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection. The objectors claim that belief without evidence is either not faith or is faith but not beneficial. The author then explains the view of those who require evidence for benefit: they argue that benefit (reward) is tied to bearing hardship (mashaqqa), and hardship is found not in the act of affirmation itself but in the effort to acquire knowledge through reflection (ta'mmul) on the evidence. Therefore, belief that comes without such effort, like at the moment of despair or in the afterlife, is not beneficial. The author responds by reiterating the Ahl al-Sunna position that reward is granted by God's favor (tafassulan) and that it corresponds to affirmation, except for cases like the faith of those who witness punishment or are compelled.

Key Terms
الاعتقاد
النفع
المشقة
الثواب
التأمل
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief, even without evidence, earns reward from God.
Correction: The objectors argue that reward is tied to the hardship of seeking evidence, so belief without evidence is not beneficial. The Ahl al-Sunna respond that reward is by God's favor and corresponds to affirmation itself, except for cases like compelled belief.

Checkpoint
According to those who require evidence for faith to be beneficial, why is belief without evidence not rewarded?
A) Because it is not true faith
B) Because it lacks the hardship of seeking evidence
C) Because it is not based on reason
D) Because it is not from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna maintain that faith itself is beneficial by God's favor, and reward corresponds to affirmation, not merely to the hardship of seeking evidence, with exceptions for compelled belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever depended on a religious act or belief for reward without examining your own effort or sincerity? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about what earns God's pleasure?
?
The objectors say hardship in seeking evidence is necessary. Do you seek evidence for your faith, or have you accepted it blindly? What does that mean for your own accountability before God?
?
If reward is ultimately by God's favor, does that make you more grateful or more complacent? What specific change does this demand from you today?
